class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">&nbsp;</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9">2.4.  Preparation of BP Anodes</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Two electrodes were fabricated, which differ for the binder component. The starting slurries were prepared through aqueous processing by mixing proper amounts of BP, carbon (Imerys Super C65), and the polymer binder with a 5/3/1 weight ratio. BP was synthesized <i>via</i> high-energy ball milling, as deeply described elsewhere. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref29" aria-label="reference 6 and 29">(6,29)</a> Raman spectra, SEM images, and XRD patterns of the as-prepared BP and the BP-C mixture used to prepare the anode slurry are reported in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/suppl_file/am0c22464_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S1–S3</a>, respectively. Two different binders were used: (i) the SH UPyPEG<sub>795</sub>UPy—PEO blend (50–50) and (ii) a blend of Na-CMC and PAA (1/1 wt/wt). In detail, BP and carbon were initially ball milled for few minutes; in a typical process, the binder was dissolved in excess water, added to the two components, and successively mixed in a planetary ball mill at 200 rpm for 2 h, followed by a 10 min stop and other 2 h of milling in reverse direction. The solid content of the slurries was kept between 12% and 15 wt %. The resulting inks were then cast on an aluminum foil (UACJ, 15 μm thick) using a doctor blade to obtain a wet film with a thickness of 100 μm that was immediately dried under vacuum at 80 °C to avoid moisture and oxygen contamination. The anode was finally cut into 2 cm<sup>2</sup> disks and stored in a glove box (MBraum, O<sub>2</sub>, H<sub>2</sub>O &lt; 0.5 ppm) before the electrochemical measurements. In the case of electrodes based on the SH blend (50–50), two BP mass loadings were explored, ∼1.26 and ∼2.5 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>; in the electrode including CMC–PAA as a binder, the mass loading was 1.6 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. Lower loadings were tested for the former system in our previous paper. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6" aria-label="reference 6">(6)</a></div></div><div id="sec2_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10">2.5.  Methods</h3><div class="NLM_p">Thermogravimetric analyses of the SH polymers were performed by heating aliquots of about 20 mg at 5 °C/min from room temperature up to 250 °C under a N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere in a Pt crucible by means of a Q5000 thermogravimetric instrument (TA Instruments, USA). Differential scanning calorimetry (DSC) analyses were performed with a Q2000 instrument (TA Instruments, USA) by heating the samples (about 20 mg) from −80 to 150 °C at 5 °C/min under a N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere in Al crucibles sealed in the glove box.</div><div class="NLM_p"><sup>1</sup>H and <sup>13</sup>C NMR high-resolution spectra were recorded on Bruker 300 and 400 MHz instruments and calibrated with the solvent residual proton signal. CHCl<sub>3</sub> was dried using 4 Å molecular sieves.</div><div class="NLM_p">Solid-state NMR measurements were acquired on a Bruker AVANCE III 400 MHz (9.4 T) equipped with a 4 mm probe at 27° ± 1 °C under MAS condition (10 kHz). <sup>1</sup>H one-pulse data were collected after T<sub>1</sub> determination to ensure the quantitative measurement condition with the use of 90<sup>o</sup> pulse of 3 μs and 16 scans; T<sub>1</sub> was evaluated with the use of a standard inversion recovery pulse sequence.  For all the samples the same experimental conditions (contact time and decoupling scheme) were used. Rotors were filled with the membranes in the same quantity by cutting the membrane in macroscopic pieces (∼2 × 2 mm); this sample preparation was necessary to obtain stable MAS rotation and likely does not change the interchain interactions under investigation.</div><div class="NLM_p">Dynamic mechanical analysis (DMA) measurements were performed on rectangular-shaped samples (35 mm × 10 mm), directly cut from the piece, by a Metravib DMA/25 equipped with a shear jaw for films. The frequency sweep experiments were carried out in the frequency range between 0.2 and 20 Hz at a constant strain of 0.004% from 20 to 60 °C every 10 °C. Temperature sweeps were performed at a heating rate of 2 °C, over a range between 20 and 60 °C, at a dynamic stress of amplitude 4 × 10<sup>–3</sup> and a frequency of 1 Hz. For the stress–strain test, the sample was clamped on the tensile module with a separation of 10 mm. The speed rate was fixed at 0.2 mm min<sup>–1</sup>. Membrane’s thickness ranged between 100 and 110 μm.</div><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical characterization of the anodes was performed by means of galvanostatic cycling and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) by using 2032-type coin cells assembled in an argon-filled glove box (H<sub>2</sub>O and O<sub>2</sub> below 0.5 ppm) with Na metal both as a counter and reference electrode. Electrodes were separated with a <i>Whatman</i> glass fiber separator (GF/D) soaked in a 1.0 M solution of NaPF<sub>6</sub> in EC/PC (50:50 vol %) with 5 wt % of NaTSFI and 2 wt % of FEC. The cells were cycled on a <i>Biologic</i> BCS 810 battery tester from 0.02 to 2 V at various C-rates (1C = 2596 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>). All the potentials reported refer to the Na<sup>+</sup>/Na couple. Rests of 48 h were typically imposed every six cycles in order to evaluate the SH effect on the cycling performances. The impedance on the cells was measured by means of EIS at room temperature by applying an AC voltage of 100 mV in the frequency range of 0.1 Hz to 1 MHz.</div><div class="NLM_p last">SEM analyses on the pristine anode and on post-mortem (both top-view and cross-section) were performed using a Tescan Mira3XMU microscope operated at 20 kV and equipped with an EDAX EDS analysis system. The samples were coated with a carbon thin film using a Cressington 208 carbon coater.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i11">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i12" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i12">3.1.  SH Binder: Material Design and Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p">As stated before, we used a chemical approach to develop a new binder for anodes in SIBs capable of SH from physical damages upon long cycling and to mitigate the huge volume expansion of the BP anode. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a> shows a naïve picture of the SH binder working with a dynamic hydrogen bonding mechanism boosted by the presence of two UPy functional groups in the backbone, which should promote a good adhesion among the BP particles themselves and also to the current collector. The SH polymer (UPyPEG<sub><i>n</i></sub>UPy) includes PEG units playing a dual role (i) to assist the Na ion transport within the anode and conferring ion-conducting properties to the binder and (ii) to decrease the charge transfer anode resistance, thus resulting in an enhanced electrochemical kinetics. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> The SH network (UPyPEG<sub><i>n</i></sub>UPy) was further blended with high-molecular weight (MW) polyethylene oxide (<i>M</i><sub>W</sub> = 300 kDa), which is physically and chemically affine, in order to achieve better free-standing properties and improve the dispersion of PB and carbon aggregates.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/asset/images/medium/am0c22464_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/asset/images/large/am0c22464_0002.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Design and working mechanism of the SH BP anode: (a) schematic representation of the binder and (b) expected functional reversible behavior of the UPyPEG<sub>795</sub>UPy—PEO blends. Red point: intercalating Na ions; black lines: repairing polymer backbones; and blue: electrode texture.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/asset/images/large/am0c22464_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c22464&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div id="sec3_1_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i13" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i13">3.1.1.  SH UPyPEG<sub><i>n</i></sub>UPy Unit</h4><div class="NLM_p">2-Ureido-4[1<i>H</i>]-pyrimidone (UPy) containing supramolecular polymers were thoroughly discussed [see, for instance, refs <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7 ref11 ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref11 ref31" aria-label="reference 7, 11 and 31">(7,11,31)</a>] in view of their excellent dynamic and supramolecular properties, including UPy-functionalized telechelics. Telechelics are the most classical of supramolecular polymers, where the highly directional physical interactions are applied by replacing some of the covalent bonds with supramolecular ones. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Here, the quadruple hydrogen bonding group (UPy), by association <i>via</i> noncovalent interactions, endcaps a preformed short polymer chain, leading to a strong increase of the virtual molecular weight and to a concurrent improvement of its mechanical and rheological properties. Such end–end associations also result in a further enhancement of the SH capability of the supramolecular polymers due to a longer linear chain extension. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33 ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 33 and 34">(33,34)</a> The preformed central segment is typically given by a thermoplastic polymer such as polybutadiene, polystyrene, polyethylene, and so forth. Other polymeric chain segments for UPy-based telechelics, such as PEG, indeed more interesting for application in LIBs and SIBs, are less explored <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The SH UPyPEG<sub><i>n</i></sub>UPy polymers were synthetized as detailed in the <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sec2" aria-label="Materials and Methods section">Materials and Methods section</a> (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="1">1</a>) by the covalent anchoring of isocyanate-functionalized UPy synthons to the terminal OH groups of PEG<sub><i>n</i></sub> (1:2 M ratio). In principle, the reaction is simple, but the process must be optimized to minimize the degree of PEG monofunctionalization. Indeed, monofunctional species can work as chain stoppers in the supramolecular polymerization of the difunctional UPy telechelics. The experimental parameters favoring the complete difunctionalization and then the telechelic formation are (i) a prolonged heating (60 °C, 48 h); (ii) the optimal molar ratio of the reagents, PEG, and isocyanate 1 (1:4); and the use of hexane as the purification agent.</div><div class="NLM_p">The obtained telechelics were first characterized by high-resolution NMR and FTIR spectroscopies. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a> shows a representative spectrum for the case of the UPyPEG<sub>795</sub>UPy sample.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/asset/images/medium/am0c22464_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/asset/images/large/am0c22464_0003.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) <sup>1</sup>H NMR spectrum (300 MHz, 25° C) of polymer 2 in CDCl<sub>3</sub>; (b) DSC thermograms of UPyPEG<sub><i>n</i></sub>UPy with different chain lengths compared to that of pure PEO<sub>6818</sub> (300 KDa). The inset shows the <i>T</i><sub>g</sub> region; (c) melting enthalpy, Δ<i>H</i><sub>m,s</sub>, and glass transition temperature, <i>T</i><sub>g</sub>, for all the investigated UPyPEG<sub><i>n</i></sub>UPy samples.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/asset/images/large/am0c22464_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c22464&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">As clearly shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a, the UPy end-capping of PEG moieties was successful. Specifically, complete difunctionalization was proven by integration of the unique proton resonances of the UPy moiety at ∼6 ppm and the unique proton resonances of the PEG moiety (namely, those of the CH<sub>2</sub> groups in the repeating unit) at ∼ 3.5 ppm. Furthermore, the polymer purity is proved by the absence of unique proton resonances of the starting material 1 (<i>e.g.</i>, the signal of CH<sub>2</sub> protons of the isocyanate moieties) (see the <sup>1</sup>H NMR spectra reported in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/suppl_file/am0c22464_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>). All the collected spectra of UPyPEG<sub><i>n</i></sub>UPy (namely, polymers 2–5 in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="1">1</a>) are fully consistent with the proposed structure for each investigated <i>n</i>, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/suppl_file/am0c22464_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>. A comparison among them also clearly provides evidence of the increasing chain length of the PEG repeating units. FTIR spectra were also collected for each UPyPEG<sub><i>n</i></sub>UPy telechelic to further confirm the expected polymer structure. The spectra do not show the vibrational signal at ∼3500 cm<sup>–1</sup> typical of the OH bond stretching, which is, in contrast, well evident in the case of pure PEG spectrum (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/suppl_file/am0c22464_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6a–e</a>). This further confirms the point that the above-described reaction took place through the foreseen mechanism.</div><div class="NLM_p">In order to select the optimal UPy telechelic in terms of chain dynamics, measurements of DSC were carried out on all the synthesized polymers. PEG is a semicrystalline polymer with glass transition temperature, <i>T</i><sub>g</sub>, well below room temperature, and this property is important to assure sufficient chain mobility and then self-repairing ability. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b compares the DSC traces of the UPyPEG<sub><i>n</i></sub>UPy networks at different <i>n</i> values. All the polymers show well-defined glass transition and melting phenomena, whose characteristic temperatures, <i>T</i><sub>g</sub> and <i>T</i><sub>m</sub>, respectively, are influenced by the PEG molecular weight. As expected, both the glass transition temperature and the melting enthalpy increase with <i>n</i>, resulting in an enhancement of polymer stiffness (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c). The UPyPEG<sub><i>n</i></sub>UPy crystalline fraction is obtained from the ratio between the experimental melting enthalpy, Δ<i>H</i><sub>m,s</sub>, and the one expected for a fully crystalline sample, assumed to be Δ<i>H</i><sub>m</sub> = 206 J g<sup>–1</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> It increases from 60% to about 85% by increasing <i>n</i>, whereas the glass transition temperatures remain below −22 °C even in the case of PEG<sub>795</sub> (<i>M</i><sub>W</sub> = 35 kDa). The thermograms also show another endothermal phenomenon between 120° and 150 °C, whose intensity decreases by increasing the PEG chain length. Such a broad signal is frequently observed in PEO-based systems and is assigned to the endothermic mixing between the crystalline PEG chain undergoing melting around 65 °C and the metastable liquid phase originating at the polymer glass transition. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The conjunction of these two properties, namely, low <i>T</i><sub>g</sub> and the presence of crystalline domains, is therefore optimal to obtain polymers with high hydrogen bonding dynamics and good mechanical strength. Taking into accounts such results, we opted for UPyPEG<sub>795</sub>UPy (<i>M</i><sub>W</sub> = 35 kDa) as the SH UPy-telechelic unit for the blend component. Here, the amorphous phase is enough to allow the rearrangement of the polymer chains and to drive the healing of any cracks autonomously. At the same time, the crystalline phase contributes to the polymer’s mechanical properties helpful to contain the large fluctuations of the BP structure in the anode. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">Finally, thermogravimetric analysis showed that each investigated telechelic is very stable from a thermal point of view with degradation temperature exceeding at least 180 °C, independently on the PEG chain length (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/suppl_file/am0c22464_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>).</div></div><div id="sec3_1_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i14" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i14">3.1.2.  SH UPyPEG<sub>795</sub>UPy—PEO Blend</h4><div class="NLM_p">The UPyPEG<sub><i>n</i></sub>UPy polymers have no optimal MW to form films with good free-standing properties. For this reason, the longer telechelic (UPyPEG<sub>795</sub>UPy) was selected to be homogeneously mixed with a higher MW polymer as PEO (300 kDa) to obtain a blend with a double function, namely, (i) SH capability ensured by the telechelic unit and (ii) good free-standing properties allowed by PEO. Specifically, three blends UPyPEG<sub>795</sub>UPy—PEO were prepared by mixing proper amounts of the single components to achieve the volume ratios (v–v) 40–60, 50–50, and 60–40. In the following, the samples will be labeled blend 40–60, blend 50–50, and blend 60–40, respectively (see <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>). For the sake of clarity, here the first number refers to UPyPEG<sub>795</sub>UPy and the second one to PEO.</div><div class="NLM_p">Initially, the SH ability of the blends was qualitatively evaluated by scratching with a razorblade 100 μm-thick films, prepared as described in the <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sec2" aria-label="Materials and Methods section">Materials and Methods section</a>, and following their spontaneous self-repairing upon time. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a shows the optical microscopy images obtained for all the investigated blends, immediately after cut (i, ii, iii) and after 2 h (i′, ii′, iii′). In the first two cases, the cracks are fully healed. In contrast, the SH of 60–40 is not recovered at all, as observed by comparing <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>iii,iii′. In order to exclude the SH effects due to the PEO component, the same test was also carried out on a pure PEO film, whose optical microscopy image is reported in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/suppl_file/am0c22464_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>, showing that no crack is repaired even after 5 days of rest time.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/asset/images/medium/am0c22464_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/asset/images/large/am0c22464_0004.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (Above) Optical microscopy images of the (UPyPEG<sub>795</sub>UPy)—PEO blends: 40–60 (i,i′), 50–50 (ii,ii′), and 60–40 (iii,iii′), just cut (above), and during the SH process (below). Bending test over time for the blends 40–60 (a,a′), 50–50 (b,b′), and 60–40 (c,c′).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/asset/images/large/am0c22464_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c22464&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The repairing ability was also checked in terms of the reconstruction of electric continuity. To this aim, a 50–50-based composite film was prepared including 15 vol % of conductive carbon (KS-10 Timcal). This film was connected to a multimeter and then cut in two pieces in order to follow the evolution with time of the film resistance. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/suppl_file/am0c22464_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a> shows the gradual and full recovery of the composite resistance after the sample crack toward the initial value shown before the rupture.</div><div class="NLM_p">In order to check the bending resistance of the blends, finally, the films were kept under forced folding for at least 2 h, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a–c. While the samples 40–60 and 60–40 underwent partial or complete rupture, 50–50 did not undergo fracture, remaining well flexible for about 4 h (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b,b′ and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/suppl_file/am0c22464_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">8b</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p">These findings can be explained by the higher crystallinity observed in the 50–50 sample, resulting in an increased mechanical robustness, as proved by the good agreement of the experimental evidence coming from our multidisciplinary approach (DSC, <sup>13</sup>C–<sup>1</sup>H solid-state NMR, and DMA), whose main results are summarized in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a compares the DSC thermograms of the three blends as prepared. Clear glass transition (see the inset) and melting phenomena are observed. While all blends exhibit a single <i>T</i><sub>g</sub>, the melting endotherms are well structured, suggesting a physical mixing of multiple phases with slightly different melting temperatures and enthalpies. Indeed, all the blends exhibit <i>T</i><sub>g</sub> and Δ<i>H</i><sub>m,s</sub> lower than those of pure UPyPEG<sub>795</sub>UPy and PEO components, resulting in semicrystalline systems where the amorphous fraction is more extended and less viscous at ambient temperature. However, 50–50 exhibits melting enthalpies of 10% and 20% higher than 40–60 and 60–40, respectively, which suggests a larger fraction of crystalline domains. As the melting enthalpy of 100% crystalline UPyPEG<sub><i>n</i></sub>UPy is not known, it is not possible to determine the amount of the crystalline fraction from DSC data, and just the trend should be considered.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/asset/images/medium/am0c22464_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/asset/images/large/am0c22464_0005.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. DSC thermograms of the (UPyPEG<sub>795</sub>UPy)—PEO blends 40–60, 50–50, and 60–40 (part a) and of the healed part of the blend 50–50 (see the inset in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>ii′). The glass transition temperatures, <i>T</i><sub>g</sub>, are highlighted in the figure insets.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/asset/images/large/am0c22464_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c22464&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b reports the DSC plot of the healed part of the 50–50 sample. Here, the several melting peaks undergo coalescence, and the glass transition temperature, <i>T</i><sub>g</sub> &lt; −34 °C, significantly decreases. Both these phenomena may be related to the formation of new dynamic H-bonds driven by the approaching of the UPy-terminated chains, which physically link the two components into a unique and highly dynamic network. The repaired damage also indicates that the presence of crystalline PEG domains does not interrupt the dynamic hydrogen bonding formation and the related properties.</div><div class="NLM_p">As for the pure components, the blends are also thermally stable at least up to 250 °C, as shown by the corresponding TGA plots, reported in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/suppl_file/am0c22464_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>.</div><div class="NLM_p">The blends were also investigated by means of solid-state NMR spectroscopy. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a shows the <sup>1</sup>H one-pulse spectra, which exhibit narrow signals at ∼4.1 ppm, overlapped to broader bands centered at the same chemical shift. Smaller features can be observed at ∼3.8 ppm and in the range of 1.9–1.3 ppm. The main peak at 4.1 ppm can be associated with the protons of the PEO units −(CH<sub>2</sub>–CH<sub>2</sub>–O)– present both in the PEO and UPy chains, in good agreement with the attribution reported for the molecule in solution (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a), and the narrow and broad contributions can be attributed to signals from the crystalline and amorphous polymer strands, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37 ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37 ref38" aria-label="reference 37 and 38">(37,38)</a> The small feature in the 3.8–3.5 ppm range, which shifts to lower fields with increasing PEO content, can be associated with the −CH<sub>2</sub>– moieties in the UPy-based polymer. Finally, the small features in the 2.5–1.3 region can be associated with the terminal methyl groups of the polymer chains, again in good agreement with the attribution reported in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38 ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38 ref39" aria-label="reference 38 and 39">(38,39)</a> The remaining protons of the UPy unit could not be observed because of their low concentration. Although the main peaks near 4 ppm are very similar, a closer inspection shows some changes with the membrane composition (see the inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a). The main part of the spectrum can be fitted in terms of three Gaussian contributions, two of them centered at 4.1 ppm and the third one ranging in the range 3.8–3.5 ppm, depending on the composition. As previously stated, this third component is associated with the CH<sub>2</sub>– moieties of the UPy-based part of the polymer. The two components at 4.1 ppm, in turn, can be attributed to system crystalline and amorphous fractions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38 ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38 ref39" aria-label="reference 38 and 39">(38,39)</a> The best fit performed on the base of this simple model shows that the amorphous/crystalline fraction does not vary linearly with the composition, but the 50–50 sample has the highest content of crystalline domains (see <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>). This trend has been qualitatively confirmed by the same analysis performed on the <sup>1</sup>H-decoupled <sup>13</sup>C spectra (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b) on the sharp and broad resonances observed for all the three samples at 71 ppm. Finally, <sup>13</sup>C–<sup>1</sup>H CPMAS spectra, reported in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c, show the same main resonance at 71 ppm due to the −(CH<sub>2</sub>–CH<sub>2</sub>–O)– moieties in the polymer chains and smaller features associated with the amide (∼175 ppm), aromatic rings (152–165 ppm), and aliphatic portions (10–50 ppm) (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c and the inset). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> Again, all the spectra are very similar. However, upon normalization to the main resonance, it is possible to observe that the 50–50 sample has a better signal-to-noise ratio than the other two compositions, which is well evident in the aliphatic portion of the spectrum (see the inset). This calls for a higher cross-polarization efficiency, which can be due to different factors, that is, the lower mobility of the interested fragments if the cross-polarization is associated with intrachain mechanisms and/or to the shorter distance among fragments if associated with the interchain ones. Both these mechanisms are compatible with higher crystallinity of this sample, which is associated with a closer packing of the polymeric chains, in agreement with the results obtained from the DSC and DMA analyses.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/asset/images/medium/am0c22464_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/asset/images/large/am0c22464_0006.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) <sup>1</sup>H spectra for the pristine blend 40–60 (blue), blend 50–50 (green), and blend 60–40 (pink) compositions; (b) <sup>13</sup>C spectra for the pristine blend 40–60 (blue), blend 50–50 (green), and blend 60–40 (pink) compositions; and (c) <sup>1</sup>H–<sup>13</sup>C CPMAS spectra for the blend 40–60 (blue), blend 50–50 (green), and blend 60–40 (pink) compositions.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/asset/images/large/am0c22464_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c22464&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">DMA was used to investigate the mechanical properties of 50–50 and 40–60 blends. The 60–40 sample was not considered due to nonefficient SH capability. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a> shows the frequency dependence of the storage (<i>G</i>′) and loss (<i>G</i>″) elastic moduli for the 50–50 (a) and 40–60 (b) samples, at 20 and 60 °C, both pristine (B50–50 and B40–60) and restored (SH-B 50–50 and SH B40–60). In the whole temperature range, all the samples reveal storage modulus higher than the loss one, which highlights their good elasticity. Nonetheless, DMA analysis underlines some crucial differences between the samples:<table class="listgroup" border="0" width="95%" id="list1" list-type="label"><tbody><tr class="li1"><td valign="top">(i)</td><td colspan="5" valign="top" class="paragraph"><p class="last">The <i>G</i>′ moduli of 50–50 is 127 MPa at 20 °C, which is roughly 30% higher than that of 40–60 gel (96 MPa), thus implying greater mechanical strength of the former blend, in agreement with the higher crystallinity revealed by DSC and NMR;</p></td></tr><tr class="li1"><td valign="top">(ii)</td><td colspan="5" valign="top" class="paragraph"><p class="last">The self-healed 50–50 sample exhibits higher elastic modulus than the pristine film. This is likely due to a larger number of strong HBs between the two components (UPyPEG<sub>795</sub>UPy and PEO) taking place in such a blend;</p></td></tr><tr class="li1"><td valign="top">(iii)</td><td colspan="5" valign="top" class="paragraph"><p class="last"><i>G</i>′ decreases upon heating (while <i>G</i>″ clearly increases) due to the gradual weakening of the polymer structure. There is also a slight dependence on the frequency of both modules for some samples, with a reduction in the <i>G</i>′/<i>G</i>″ ratio below 10, which is typical of “weak gel”-like systems. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a></p></td></tr></tbody></table></div><figure id="fig6" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/asset/images/medium/am0c22464_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/asset/images/large/am0c22464_0007.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a,b) Frequency sweep tests, at 20 and 60 °C, for the (UPyPEG<sub>795</sub>UPy)—PEO blends: (a) blend 50–50 both pristine B50–50 and self-healed SH-B50–50 and (b) blend 40–60 both pristine B40–60 and self-healed SH-B40–60. (c,d) Master curves of storage (<i>G</i>′) and loss (<i>G</i>″) moduli reduced at a reference temperature of 40 °C: (c) blend 50–50 both pristine B50–50 and self-healed SH-B50–50; (d) blend 40–60, both pristine B40–60, and self-healed SH-B40–60. (e) Temperature evolution of the storage moduli (<i>G</i>′) from 25 to 60 °C performed on pristine blend 50–50 (B50–50), self-healed blend (SH-B50–50), blend 40–60 (B40–60), and self-healed blend SH-B40–60. (f) Stress–strain plot for blend 50–50, both pristine B50–50 and self-healed SH-B50–50; and blend 40–60, both pristine B40–60 and self-healed SH-B40–60.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/asset/images/large/am0c22464_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c22464&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">With regard to this last point, a different behavior is observed in the case of self-healed 50–50 blend, which is able to maintain the mechanical properties of a “strong gel”-like system (both modules independent from ω and with a <i>G</i>′/<i>G</i>″ ratio &gt;10) even at relatively high temperature (<i>i.e.</i>, 60 °C). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> In a nutshell, the SH process enhances the mechanical strength as well as the thermostability of the 50:50 blend.</div><div class="NLM_p">Master curves were constructed by using the “time–temperature superposition (TTS)” principle, based on the Williams–Landel–Ferry model. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> This allows the prediction of the mechanical behavior of polymers as a function of frequency over time scales that are not experimentally accessible. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44 ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44 ref45" aria-label="reference 44 and 45">(44,45)</a> For each sample, the frequency sweeps acquired at five test temperatures (from 20 to 60 °C every 10 °C) were shifted to the reference temperature of 40 °C, allowing to extend the frequency window from 0.01 mHz to 0.1 MHz (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c,d). In such a wide frequency range, all the membranes show an elastic solid-like behavior since the elastic module <i>G</i>′ is averagely 10 times larger than the viscous module <i>G</i>″. However, both the two 40–60 membranes and the pristine 50–50 one show module values approaching toward the low-frequency region, which is an indication of a weakening of the polymeric film. Accordingly, a clear loss of elasticity occurs to the systems as a consequence of a “<i>strong–weak</i>” network transition. Despite this, no crossover between the moduli was found in the whole frequency range, implying the absence of “<i>gel</i>–<i>sol</i>” transition. This indicates that the blends possess excellent stability over a wide range of time scales, either in undamaged or in self-healed state. These master curves also confirm the exceptional mechanical properties of the 50–50 restored membrane, which maintains a strong gel behavior on a wide range of time scales.</div><div class="NLM_p">The temperature sweep tests on a heating–cooling cycle are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>e, where the temperature behavior of the storage modulus (<i>G</i>′), in the range 25–60 °C on the first heating and cooling scan cycle, is displayed. All the samples exhibit a similar behavior: <i>G</i>′ slightly decreases during heating, but it is almost completely recovered on cooling. Actually, a certain hysteresis is observed, which means that more time, less than 1 h, is needed to fully recover the module to its initial value in cooling. Indeed, the mechanical spectra executed on more thermal cycles are practically superimposable (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/suppl_file/am0c22464_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>), clearly indicating the thermoreversibility of these systems.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Finally, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>f shows the stress–strain behavior of the pristine and self-healed 50–50 and 40–60 membranes up to the limit of their respective linearity region. Undamaged blends exhibit similar tensile stress (<i>ca.</i> 17.5 MPa), but the elongation at yield point is higher for 40–60 (1.66%) than for 50–50 (1.33%). Such higher elasticity is likely due to the rubber-like properties of the network. As can be clearly seen, both the healed polymer films can effectively self-recover to their original strain after a healing duration of 24 h at room temperature. It is worth noting that the self-healed 50–50 polymer can sustain a remarkably higher tensile stress, that is, <i>ca.</i> 22 MPa, than the undamaged membrane, <i>ca.</i> 17 MPa. Such a massive increase in membrane strength is clearly compatible with the formation of an efficient hydrogen-bonded crosslinked structure.</div></div></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i15" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i15">3.2.  Battery Cycling</h3><div class="NLM_p">To the best of our knowledge, contrary to the silicon-based anodes, chemical SH of BP-based anodes for LIBs or SIBs has not been explored yet. As described in previous reports, the huge volume expansion (up to 300%) observed in this materials upon cycling is typically managed by means of BP composites with high amount of carbon, which acts as a volume buffer. Such a strategy is in principle promising but causes a decrease of the volumetric energy density and the success depends on several variables, including the type of carbon, anode composition, electrolyte, and additives. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3" aria-label="reference 3">(3)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">This work describes, for the first time, the use of a novel binder with SH ability in the aqueous processing of BP anodes for SIBs. To this aim, the blend 50–50 (namely, UPyPEG<sub>795</sub>UPy—PEO 50–50 volume ratio) was selected as the SH polymeric component of the electrode due to its excellent elastic properties and recovering ability. To properly evaluate the repairing effect of such a binder on the electrochemical behavior of BP in SIBs, the functional properties of the anode were compared with those observed for the same anode including a conventional CMC–PAA binder. The galvanostatic behavior was evaluated on coin cells from 0.01 and 2 V <i>versus</i> Na<sup>+</sup>/Na at different current densities by using sodium as the counterelectrode.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>a,b reports the rate performances and the corresponding voltage profiles of the BP anodes made with the SH and the CMC–PAA binders. The BP mass loading in the anode was 1.26 and 1.6 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>, respectively. Electrodes including different BP amounts were also investigated, namely, 2.5 mg cm<sup>–2</sup> for B50–50 and 1 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>, but no significant differences were observed in the electrochemical performances (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/suppl_file/am0c22464_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a> ad ref  <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6" aria-label="reference 6">(6)</a>). On cycling, the first cycle discharge (desodiation) capacity is 2450 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.18 A g<sup>–1</sup> (corresponding to a cycling rate of C/20 based on the theoretical capacity of BP 1C = 2596 mA h g<sup>–13</sup>), very close to the theoretical one for both the anodes. Upon sodiation, both the samples show a typical stepwise process related to the formation of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>P species, followed by a pronounced plateau at around 0.3 V <i>versus</i> Na metal, due to the final Na<sub>3</sub>P formation. After the first cycle, the discharge capacity of SH anodes decreases up to 1700 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, as typically observed in these systems. However, starting from the second cycle, the cycling stability of the two electrodes is dramatically different. After 25 cycles at the same current density (0.185 A g<sup>–1</sup>), the BP anode with a 50–50 binder retained ∼70% of the initial capacity, proving the SH agent ability to assure stronger adhesion among the phosphorus particles, thus mitigating structural instability. In contrast, the capacity of the anode with a CMC–PAA binder abruptly drops to about 100 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after the first five cycles, corresponding to a capacity retention less than 10%, in agreement with what is generally observed for pure BP anodes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref46" aria-label="reference 6 and 46">(6,46)</a></div><figure id="fig7" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/asset/images/medium/am0c22464_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/asset/images/large/am0c22464_0008.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. (a) Comparison of the rate performances of two BP anodes (0.05 C: <i>I</i> = 185 mA g<sup>–1</sup>; 0.1C: <i>I</i> = 371 mA g<sup>–1</sup>; 0.2C: <i>I</i> = 742 mA g<sup>–1</sup>; and 1C: 3708 mA g<sup>–1</sup>), including the SH binder (circles, blue: discharge; red: charge; and light green: efficiency) and the conventional CMC–PAA (squares, black: discharge; gray: charge; and dark green: efficiency), (b) voltage profiles of the SH BP anode, (c) voltage profiles upon time at C/20 or 185 mA g<sup>–1</sup> including a rest period of 48 h, and (d) Nyquist plots collected during and at the end of the galvanostatic cycling tests on the SH BP anode.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/asset/images/large/am0c22464_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c22464&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The impressive stabilizing effects of the 50–50 SH binder is further confirmed when this anode is kept to rest for 48 h (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>a,c). Contrary to the anode with CMC–PAA, after the cycling breaks, the SH anode regains about 80% of the capacity lost during the first 25 cycles at 0.185 A h g<sup>–1</sup> (increasing from 1000 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> to 1500 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>). This demonstrates the maintenance of a good electric contact upon cycling, which is secured by the autonomic healing of the mechanical cracks through the UPy-driven multiple hydrogen bonding. The repairing ability of the binder also affects the electrode cycling performances. In fact, as the current density increases, the specific capacity only slightly decreases, achieving 1000 and 850 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.371 A g<sup>–1</sup> (C/10) and 0.742 A g<sup>–1</sup>(C/5), respectively, with Coulombic efficiency very close to unit. Although a definite plateau in the capacity behavior is not reached, as observed in the case of SH pure Si anodes, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18 ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref22" aria-label="reference 18 and 22">(18,22)</a> the binder exhibits an impressive stabilizing effect even at high current densities. In fact, the SH BP anode still works well around 500 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. This result is better than those observed in the case of other mitigation strategies, for instance, the introduction of Ge into BP to enhance the elastic softness, for which similar capacity retention was only observed at a 4 times lower current density. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">EIS was used to check the SH effect on the interfacial resistances (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>d). The spectra were obtained at the 20th cycle (i) before, (ii) after the 48 h rest period, and (iii) at the end of the cycling test. The charge transfer resistance is in principle very low in both cases and undergoes further decrease in consequence of the rest from 45 to 15 Ω. This trend indicates the growth on the anode of a stable, thin, and low-resistance SEI, which is able to protect the BP surface avoiding abrupt capacity decays.</div><div class="NLM_p">In the case of CMC–PAA-based BP anode, charge transfer resistances much higher than those measured for the SH electrode were obtained by impedance spectroscopy measurements (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/suppl_file/am0c22464_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13</a>) after electrochemical cycling, namely, 1840 and 1221 Ω, respectively. The spectrum at the end of the cycling test appears more complex, likely because of the formation of one or more different passivation layers, reasonably thicker than what, in contrast, noticed in the B50–50 anode.</div><div class="NLM_p">Finally, both top-view and cross-sectional scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>) were collected on the cell before the galvanostatic cycling and at the end of the whole tests (after 55 cycles at different current densities). The top-view images (part a) provide evidence that the SH anode displays a much more flat and smooth morphology with respect to the CMC–PAA-based electrode, which shows even a much discontinuous surface. This low rugosity is also maintained after electrochemical cycling, where no significant increase of roughness is noted. A texture of small grains  distributed on the surface is also present reasonably related to the SEI formation and byproducts of electrolyte decomposition. Higher magnification of the SEM top-view images is included in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/suppl_file/am0c22464_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14</a> of the Supporting Information. The cross-sectional images (part b) show that the BP anode including the SH binder is able to preserve the electrode morphology. The cross-section of the pristine anode (a) reveals a slightly porous electrode with a thickness of about 35 μm and a homogeneous distribution of the particle sizes. Despite such low porosity, the Na migration along the anode is still ensured thanks to a polymer chain-assisted mechanism, allowed by the low <i>T</i><sub>g</sub>-PEG-based SH binder, further plasticized by the carbonate liquid electrolytes. Except for a slight increase of porosity, the cycled electrode does not reveal any significant structural degradation or reduction of adhesion with the current collector, which is still very good, contrary to what is observed in the case of CMC–PAA binders where post-mortem SEM-FIB/EDX revealed serious coating layer detachments from the Al foil, as already shown in our previous paper. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6" aria-label="reference 6">(6)</a> In addition, the cycled electrode thickness does not increase, confirming that such a binder prevents the BP anode from irreversible volume expansion thanks to its SH ability.</div><figure id="fig8" data-index="9" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/asset/images/medium/am0c22464_0009.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/asset/images/large/am0c22464_0009.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. (a) Top-view images of the CMC–PAA-based (up) and SH-based (down) BP anode before and after cycling; (b) cross-sectional SEM images of the pristine SH-based BP anode (up), and of the same electrode observed after the end of galvanostatic cycling tests (down).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/asset/images/large/am0c22464_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c22464&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i16">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">For the first time to the best of our knowledge, we have reported on a novel and sustainable polymeric blend with SH ability as a binder in BP-based anodes for SIBs. The repairing reactivity is intrinsic and based on dynamic multiple hydrogen bonding enabled by UPy-telechelic networks. Specifically, we have successfully demonstrated that the SH properties of the binder have remarkable beneficial effects on both cycling performances and stability of the electrode in Na-ion cells.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Although additional work is necessary to meet the commercial requirements of SIBs or LIBs, undoubtedly the SH performances of this binder are very promising and will be further explored. In particular, our results lay the groundwork to extend the use of the UPy-telechelic backbones also to other electrochemically active materials suffering huge volumetric changes, even in synergy with other strategies which already proved their ability to improve the structural stability, for example, carbon as a buffering agent.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i17">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.0c22464?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.0c22464</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Raman Spectra, SEM, and XRD of BP; <sup>1</sup>H NMR of compound 1 and polymer 2; <sup>1</sup>H NMR of polymers 2–5 and <sup>13</sup>C NMR of polymer 4,5; FTIR spectra of pure PEG and polymers 2–5; TGA of polymers 2–5; image of optical microscopy of crack on pure PEO and photo of blend 40–50 after 4 h of bending test; electric SH test; TGA of the investigated blends; additional DMA analysis of the blend 50–50 and 40–60; galvanostatic cycling of SH BP anode at different mass loadings; Nyquist plots of SH BP anode before cycling and of CMC–PAA-based BP anode both before and after cycling; and SEM top view images of both SH and CMC–PAA BP anode before and after cycling tests (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/suppl_file/am0c22464_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c22464/suppl_file/am0c22464_si_001.pdf">am0c22464_si_001.pdf (1.82 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.0c22464" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Promise and reality of post-lithium-ion batteries with high energy densities</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Choi, Jang Wook; Aurbach, Doron</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Reviews Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">1</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">16013</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy d. is the main property of rechargeable batteries that has driven the entire technol. forward in past decades.  Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) now surpass other, previously competitive battery types (for example, lead-acid and nickel metal hydride) but still require extensive further improvement to, in particular, extend the operation hours of mobile IT devices and the driving mileages of all-elec. vehicles.  In this Review, we present a crit. overview of a wide range of post-LIB materials and systems that could have a pivotal role in meeting such demands.  We divide battery systems into two categories: near-term and long-term technologies.  To provide a realistic and balanced perspective, we describe the operating principles and remaining issues of each post-LIB technol., and also evaluate these materials under com. cell configurations.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpmrufRRuizrLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh8wLfUO2gR9w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhtVert7k%253D&amp;md5=3d2782c5ebf801e43442f01f2206379f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2016.07.023"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Casimir, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ogoke, O.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Amine, J.                         cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Silicon-based anodes for lithium-ion batteries: Effectiveness of materials synthesis and electrode preparation</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Casimir, Anix; Zhang, Hanguang; Ogoke, Ogechi; Amine, Joseph C.; Lu, Jun; Wu, Gang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">27</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">359-376</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NEANCA</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2211-2855</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Lithium-ion batteries are widely used throughout the world for portable electronic devices and mobile phones and show great potential for more demanding applications like elec. vehicles.  Unfortunately, lithium-ion batteries still lack the required level of energy storage to completely meet the demands of such applications as elec. vehicles.  Among advanced materials being studied, silicon nanoparticles have demonstrated great potential as an anode material to replace the commonly used graphite.  Silicon has been shown to have a high theor. gravimetric capacity, approx. 4200 mA h/g, compared to only 372 mA h/g for graphite.  Though silicon nanoparticles have remarkably high capacity, they suffer from rapid degrdn. with each cycle due to electrode vol. expansion of approx. 400% during lithiation, placing a large strain on the material.  With each cycle that strain creates cracks in the electrode particles and causes them to break down into smaller particles, which create void spaces between the particles and lead to poor contact as reflected in poor cond.  In this review, we discuss exciting new research on silicon-based anodes conducted during the past couple of years.  Besides stressing the importance of well-designed nanostructures of Si, we focus on optimization of the Si electrode and resulting performance enhancement by properly selecting binders and synergistically integrating them with various carbon materials during electrode design and fabrication.  Importantly, although each improvement strategy has its own advantage, appropriate combination of them will yield much higher anode performance.  We summarize the core issues in developing the silicon anode and effective strategies in yielding more promising results.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGranonB1DzdKbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh8wLfUO2gR9w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28Xht12nsrjK&amp;md5=80307ce2bdcff0e95e389526f6e535ca</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201702849"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Advanced Phosphorus-Based Materials for Lithium/Sodium-Ion Batteries: Recent Developments and Future Perspectives</span>. <i>Adv.                         "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1007/s41918-018-00028-w"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dou, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shi, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, D.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Silicon/Carbon Composite Anode Materials for Lithium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Electrochem. Energy Rev.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>2</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">149</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">198</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1007/s41918-018-00028-w</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D2%26publication_year%3D2019%26pages%3D149-198%26journal%3DElectrochem.%2BEnergy%2BRev.%26author%3DF.%2BDou%26author%3DL.%2BShi%26author%3DG.%2BChen%26author%3DD.%2BZhang%26title%3DSilicon%252FCarbon%2BComposite%2BAnode%2BMaterials%2Bfor%2BLithium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1007%252Fs41918-018-00028-w&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.0c22464&amp;doiOfLink=10.1007%2Fs41918-018-00028-w&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Silicon/Carbon Composite Anode Materials for Lithium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Dou, Fei; Shi, Liyi; Chen, Guorong; Zhang, Dengsong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochemical Energy Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">2</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">149-198</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EERLAM</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2520-8489</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Springer International Publishing AG</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review on, silicon (Si) is a representative anode material for next-generation lithium-ion batteries due to properties such as a high theor. capacity, suitable working voltage, and high natural abundance.  However, due to inherently large vol. expansions (∼ 400%) during insertion/deinsertion processes as well as poor elec. cond. and unstable solid electrolyte interfaces (SEI) films, Si-based anodes possess serious stability problems, greatly hindering practical application.  To resolve these issues, the modification of Si anodes with carbon (C) is a promising method which has been demonstrated to enhance elec. cond. and material plasticity.  In this review, recent researches into Si/C anodes are grouped into categories based on the structural dimension of Si materials, including nanoparticles, nanowires and nanotubes, nanosheets, and porous Si-based materials, and the structural and electrochem. performance of various Si/C composites based on carbon materials with varying structures will be discussed.  In addn., the progress and limitations of the design of existing Si/C composite anodes are summarized, and future research perspectives in this field are presented.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrj74qgWCS147Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh8wLfUO2gR9w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXitVaksbnO&amp;md5=7d3a9cce4c203d70f050d3a40f9b3055</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1002/cnma.201900708"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hou, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, R.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Silicon Anodes for High-Performance Storage Devices: Structural Design, Material Compounding, Advances in Electrolytes and Binders</span>. <i>ChemNanoMat</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>6</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">720</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">738</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Silicon Anodes for High-Performance Storage Devices: Structural Design, Material Compounding, Advances in Electrolytes and Binders</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Fangru; Xu, Jie; Hou, Zhiwei; Li, Min; Yang, Ru</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ChemNanoMat</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Silicon has an extremely high theor. capacity, a low voltage platform, and abundant natural reserves.  It is considered to be one of the most promising anode materials for lithium-ion batteries.  However, there are still many problems with silicon as an anode material for lithium-ion batteries.  During the lithiation/delithiation of silicon, a huge vol. expansion occurs, causing the silicon particles to pulverize and the capacity to drop sharply.  Furthermore, the continuous increase in the silicon surface solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) films and the poor cond. of silicon affect the specific capacity of the battery.  Means to improve silicon-based anodes with regard to electrode cycling performance and electrode capacity include structural design, composite prepn., or improvements in electrolytes and binders (for example, designing silicon nanomaterials of different dimensions from 0D to 3D, including silicon nanoparticles, nanowires, nanorods, thin films, porous silicon, and core-shell structures).  Silicon has been combined with different materials to buffer its own vol. expansion, resulting in excellent performance.  In addn., the design of a reasonable electrolyte soln., as well as the development of a self-healing binder is one of the main methods to improve Si-based anodes.  In recent years, sodium/potassium ion batteries have been increasingly studied, and silicon and sodium/potassium can be alloyed.  The corresponding theor. capacity can reach 954 mAh g-1 and 955 mAh g-1, resp.  Advances in silicon-based anodes for sodium/potassium ion storage are summarized herein.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpwqXxLzfOtabVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lg7fVZe3DxmlQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXoslemsLk%253D&amp;md5=bd16fe94955fe7aa0de8105a58a46f75</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1149/1945-7111/ab856e"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Quartarone, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Eisenmann, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kuenzel, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tealdi, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Marrani, A.                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Towards advanced sodium-ion batteries: green, low-cost and high-capacity anode compartment encompassing phosphorus/carbon nanocomposite as the active material and aluminum as the current collector</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Quartarone, E.; Eisenmann, T.; Kuenzel, M.; Tealdi, C.; Marrani, A.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">IOP Publishing Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are promising alternative to Lithium-ion batteries for massive stationary energy storage.  To improve energy d., however, more performing active materials are needed.  In order to allow sustainable scale-up, it is also mandatory to develop green products and processes.  Herein, we report on anodes of phosphorus/carbon (P/C) nanocomposites prepd. via High Energy Ball Milling (HEBM), a simple, powerful and easily scalable synthesis technique.  The electrodes were prepd. under oxygen-free atm., using water as solvent, which enabled the use of aluminum (instead of copper) as current collector, implying significant cost redn.  The P/C nanocomposite obtained after 54 h HEBM resulted in excellent cycling stability, delivering very high specific capacity (2200 mAh g-1, C/20) and showing good capacity retention after 120 cycles.  A careful structural anal. (XRD, FESEM-EDS, XPS), revealed that long milling times strongly increased cycling stability due to: (i) significant decrease of P particle size inside the matrix and deep composite amorphization, which alleviates the buffering dimensional issues typical of black phosphorus; (ii) presence of defects in the carbonaceous component, which allows easier Na+ insertion into the anode.  Our results show that P/C nanocomposites are very promising anode materials for SIBs, paving the way for further exploitation of nano-architectures in SIBs technol.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpAoAL3EVqpY7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lg7fVZe3DxmlQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXhvFSku7fJ&amp;md5=beadb6557a28c89d73a487117ebd9901</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1038/s41578-020-0202-4"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Urban, M.                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Self-healing polymers</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Siyang; Urban, Marek W.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Reviews Materials</span>
