Energy Rev.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>2</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">149</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">198</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1007/s41918-018-00028-w</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Silicon/Carbon Composite Anode Materials for Lithium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Dou, Fei; Shi, Liyi; Chen, Guorong; Zhang, Dengsong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochemical Energy Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">2</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">149-198</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EERLAM</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2520-8489</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Springer International Publishing AG</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review on, silicon (Si) is a representative anode material for next-generation lithium-ion batteries due to properties such as a high theor. capacity, suitable working voltage, and high natural abundance.  However, due to inherently large vol. expansions (∼ 400%) during insertion/deinsertion processes as well as poor elec. cond. and unstable solid electrolyte interfaces (SEI) films, Si-based anodes possess serious stability problems, greatly hindering practical application.  To resolve these issues, the modification of Si anodes with carbon (C) is a promising method which has been demonstrated to enhance elec. cond. and material plasticity.  In this review, recent researches into Si/C anodes are grouped into categories based on the structural dimension of Si materials, including nanoparticles, nanowires and nanotubes, nanosheets, and porous Si-based materials, and the structural and electrochem. performance of various Si/C composites based on carbon materials with varying structures will be discussed.  In addn., the progress and limitations of the design of existing Si/C composite anodes are summarized, and future research perspectives in this field are presented.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrj74qgWCS147Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh98wk2yHPjWA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXitVaksbnO&amp;md5=7d3a9cce4c203d70f050d3a40f9b3055</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref5" class="rightTabRefNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit5" data-doi="10.1002/cnma.201900708"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hou, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, R.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Silicon Anodes for High-Performance Storage Devices: Structural Design, Material Compounding, Advances in Electrolytes and Binders</span>. <i>ChemNanoMat</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>6</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">720</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">738</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/cnma.201900708</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Silicon Anodes for High-Performance Storage Devices: Structural Design, Material Compounding, Advances in Electrolytes and Binders</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Fangru; Xu, Jie; Hou, Zhiwei; Li, Min; Yang, Ru</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ChemNanoMat</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">720-738</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CHEMSB</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2199-692X</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Silicon has an extremely high theor. capacity, a low voltage platform, and abundant natural reserves.  It is considered to be one of the most promising anode materials for lithium-ion batteries.  However, there are still many problems with silicon as an anode material for lithium-ion batteries.  During the lithiation/delithiation of silicon, a huge vol. expansion occurs, causing the silicon particles to pulverize and the capacity to drop sharply.  Furthermore, the continuous increase in the silicon surface solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) films and the poor cond. of silicon affect the specific capacity of the battery.  Means to improve silicon-based anodes with regard to electrode cycling performance and electrode capacity include structural design, composite prepn., or improvements in electrolytes and binders (for example, designing silicon nanomaterials of different dimensions from 0D to 3D, including silicon nanoparticles, nanowires, nanorods, thin films, porous silicon, and core-shell structures).  Silicon has been combined with different materials to buffer its own vol. expansion, resulting in excellent performance.  In addn., the design of a reasonable electrolyte soln., as well as the development of a self-healing binder is one of the main methods to improve Si-based anodes.  In recent years, sodium/potassium ion batteries have been increasingly studied, and silicon and sodium/potassium can be alloyed.  The corresponding theor. capacity can reach 954 mAh g-1 and 955 mAh g-1, resp.                         rightTab-cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Towards advanced sodium-ion batteries: green, low-cost and high-capacity anode compartment encompassing phosphorus/carbon nanocomposite as the active material and aluminum as the current collector</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Quartarone, E.; Eisenmann, T.; Kuenzel, M.; Tealdi, C.; Marrani, A. G.; Brutti, S.; Callegari, D.; Passerini, S.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">IOP Publishing Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are promising alternative to Lithium-ion batteries for massive stationary energy storage.  To improve energy d., however, more performing active materials are needed.  In order to allow sustainable scale-up, it is also mandatory to develop green products and processes.  Herein, we report on anodes of phosphorus/carbon (P/C) nanocomposites prepd. via High Energy Ball Milling (HEBM), a simple, powerful and easily scalable synthesis technique.  The electrodes were prepd. under oxygen-free atm., using water as solvent, which enabled the use of aluminum (instead of copper) as current collector, implying significant cost redn.  The P/C nanocomposite obtained after 54 h HEBM resulted in excellent cycling stability, delivering very high specific capacity (2200 mAh g-1, C/20) and showing good capacity retention after 120 cycles.  A careful structural anal. (XRD, FESEM-EDS, XPS), revealed that long milling times strongly increased cycling stability due to: (i) significant decrease of P particle size inside the matrix and deep composite amorphization, which alleviates the buffering dimensional issues typical of black phosphorus; (ii) presence of defects in the carbonaceous component, which allows easier Na+ insertion into the anode.  Our results show that P/C nanocomposites are very promising anode materials for SIBs, paving the way for further exploitation of nano-architectures in SIBs technol.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpAoAL3EVqpY7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh98wk2yHPjWA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXhvFSku7fJ&amp;md5=beadb6557a28c89d73a487117ebd9901</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref7" class="rightTabRefNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit7" data-doi="10.1038/s41578-020-0202-4"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Urban, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Abstr.: Self-healing is the capability of a material to recover from phys. damage.  Both phys. and chem. approaches have been used to construct self-healing polymers.  These include diffusion and flow, shape-memory effects, heterogeneous self-healing systems, covalent-bond reformation and reshuffling, dynamics of supramol. chem. or combinations thereof.  In this Review, we discuss the similarities and differences between approaches to achieve self-healing in synthetic polymers, where possible placing this discussion in the context of biol. systems.  In particular, we highlight the role of thermal transitions, network heterogeneities, localized chem. reactions enabling the reconstruction of damage and phys. reshuffling.  We also discuss energetic and length-scale considerations, as well as scientific and technol. challenges and opportunities.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrhJMm9D6GaEbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh98wk2yHPjWA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXhtlSktLrI&amp;md5=c285a80834858f0f808e83a26c73affa</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref8" class="rightTabRefNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit8" data-doi="10.1039/c3cs60109a"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Urban, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Inspired by nature, self-healing materials represent the forefront of recent developments in materials chem. and engineering.  This review outlines the recent advances in the field of self-healing polymers.  The first part discusses thermodn. requirements for self-healing networks in the context of conformation changes that contribute to the Gibbs free energy.  The chain flexibility significantly contributes to the entropy changes, whereas the heat of reaction and the external energy input are the main contributors to enthalpy changes.  The second part focuses on chem. reactions that lead to self-healing, and the primary classes are the covalent bonding, supramol. assemblies, ionic interactions, chemo-mech. self-healing, and shape memory polymers.  The third part outlines recent advances using encapsulation, remote self-healing and the role of shape memory polymers.  Recent developments in the field of self-healing polymers undeniably indicate that the main challenge will be the designing of high glass transition (Tg) functional materials, which also exhibit stimuli-responsive attributes.  Build-in controllable hierarchical heterogeneousness at various length scales capable of remote self-healing by phys. and chem. responses will be essential in designing future materials of the 21st century.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrrt2wC-OSawLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh98wk2yHPjWA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXht1Wisb%252FO&amp;md5=3a1082d1d58eadf7b424bd1d32c8e203</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref9" class="rightTabRefNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit9" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201813525"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chakma, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Konkolewicz, D.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Dynamic Covalent Bonds in Polymeric Materials</span>. <i>Angew.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Dynamic covalent bonds (DCBs) have received significant attention over the past decade.  These are covalent bonds that are capable of exchanging or switching between several mols.  Particular focus has recently been on utilizing these DCBs in polymeric materials.  Introduction of DCBs into a polymer material provides it with powerful properties including self-healing, shape-memory properties, increased toughness, and ability to relax stresses as well as to change from one macromol. architecture to another.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Major improvements in stability and performance of batteries are still required for a more effective diffusion in industrial key sectors such as automotive and foldable electronics.  An encouraging route resides in the implementation into energy storage devices of self-healing features, which can effectively oppose the deterioration upon cycling that is typical of these devices.  In order to provide a comprehensive view of the topic, this Review first summarizes the main self-healing processes that have emerged in the multifaceted field of smart materials, classifying them on the basis of their recovering mechanisms.  Then, attention is closely focused on self-healable energy storage devices.  In particular, self-healing in lithium-ion and lithium-metal batteries is discussed, emphasizing both the phys. (cracks, fractures, cuts, etc.) and chem. (degrdn., gas prodn., etc.) issues that currently threaten the operating life of these devices, and the more effective self-healing strategies which can prevent or postpone undesired and dangerous failures.  Finally, an outlook on the possible resoln. of relevant challenges is briefly discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGodITxp0swANLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljBNWXRS1YmCw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXitFOgu7jO&amp;md5=321f21ffaa8c8b7943b6df7479e1aa4e</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref15" class="rightTabRefNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit15" data-doi="10.1016/j.joule.2018.02.012"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nuli, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hirano, S.-i.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Silicon Microparticle Anodes with Self-Healing Multiple Network Binder</span>. <i>Joule</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>2</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">950</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">961</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Si has been regarded as a promising anode material for Li-ion batteries.  However, its dramatic vol. change during cycling poses formidable challenges to building stable electrodes.  Since Si microparticles (SiMPs) can easily cause the pulverization and loss of electronic contact, many previous works were focused on porous-structured, nanostructured, or hierarchically structured silicon materials to improve the cycle stability.  However, their low-cost and mass prodn. is mostly difficult or even impossible. Moreover, its multiple network structure with rigid-soft chains and bonds, and special self-healing capability in situ formed during the electrode prepn., not only provides enough mech. support but also buffers the strain caused by the vol. change of SiMPs.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The overwhelming advantage of Si nanoparticles (SiNPs) over Si microsized particles (SiMPs) as anode materials is compromised by their severe side reactions with electrolyte and agglomeration during cycling.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The ability to repair damage spontaneously, which is termed self-healing, is an important survival feature because it increases the lifetime of most living creatures.  This feature is highly desirable for rechargeable batteries because the lifetime of high-capacity electrodes, such as Si anodes, is shortened by mech. fractures generated during the cycling process.  Here, inspired by nature, the authors apply self-healing chem. to Si microparticle (SiMP) anodes to overcome their short cycle-life.  Anodes made from low-cost SiMPs (∼3-8 μm), for which stable deep galvanostatic cycling was previously impossible, can now have an excellent cycle life when coated with a self-healing polymer.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Natural polymers with abundant side functionalities are emerging as a promising binder for high-capacity yet large-vol.-change silicon anodes with a strong and reversible supramol. interaction that originates from secondary bonding.  However, the supramol. network solely based on hydrogen bonding is relatively vulnerable to repeated deformation and has an insufficient diffusivity of lithium ions.  Herein, reported is a facile but efficient way of incorporating the natural polymers with an ionically conductive crosslinker, which can construct a robust network for silicon anodes.  The boronic acid in the crosslinker spontaneously reacts with natural polymers to generate boronic esters at room temp. without any kind of triggers, which gives a strong and dynamic covalent bonding to the supramol. network.  The other component in the crosslinker, polyethylene oxide, contributes to the enhanced ionic cond. of polymers, leading to outstanding rate performances even at a high mass loading of silicon nanoparticles (&gt;2 mg cm-2).         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Polymer binders as a crit. component in rechargeable batteries provide the electrodes with interconnected structures and mech. strength to maintain the electronic/ionic transfer during battery cycling.  The conventional binders, such as polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF), are not ideal candidates due to their relatively low adhesiveness, weak mech. strength, and poor functionality for high-energy-d. batteries with a thick electrode and/or a high-capacity electrode.  Nevertheless, the binder has not yet received the attention that reflects its importance in batteries.  The requirements of high energy d. batteries make essential the exploration of advanced polymeric binders, which possess particular functions.  In this review, state-of-the-art binder design strategies are sorted in terms of the challenges of various electrodes (anodes and cathodes for lithium ion batteries (LIBs) and sulfur cathodes for Li-S batteries), which have been commercialized or have the potential for practical cells.  A deep insight into how the polymeric binders improve the cell performance and the design principle of new binders is also provided.  Finally, a perspective on the direction of future binder development for high-energy-d. batteries with long cycle life is presented.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpNdWkavMAIKrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh38HWIf6AIQw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXitVKrsL%252FE&amp;md5=946f97aa99ff519d9bf96127f199e25a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref21" class="rightTabRefNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit21" data-doi="10.1002/chem.201900988"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pan, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, P.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Silicon (Si) is a promising candidate for high-capacity anode materials owing to its high theor. capacity (3579 mAh g-1), low working voltage, and wide natural abundance, although its huge vol. variation during charge/discharge processes always results in a short cycling life.  Polymer binders play a vital role in improving the cycling performance of Si-based anodes, although traditional polyvinylidene difluoride cannot fulfil the requirements owing to its weak van der Waals forces with the Si surface.  Recently, polymer binders constructed by dynamic bonds have been developed, which are reported to allow high-energy-d. electrodes with improved electrochem. performance.  With dynamic bonds including hydrogen bonding, ionic bonding, and host-guest interactions, these polymer binders possess self-healing capabilities and enhanced mech. performance, achieving a tremendous advance in addressing the capacity fading of Si-based anodes.  In this review, we will summarize the research progress of polymer binders constructed with dynamic bonds, and the challenges for their real applications in advanced Li-ion batteries will also be discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrCq3aK6YIgMbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh38HWIf6AIQw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXht1ynurzF&amp;md5=75d7305f0b035299be2f15999b59ef7a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref22"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref22" class="rightTabRefNumLink">22</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit22" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201703138"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Munaoka, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lopez, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">To, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A novel supramol. network-reinforced and self-healing composite polymer electrolyte (SHCPE) is designed and fabricated via the incorporation of ureido-pyrimidinone (UPy)-functionalized SiO2 (SiO2-UPy) into the polymer matrix contg. UPy units.  The bonded UPy groups on SiO2 could form supramol. networks with the polymer via the interactions of quadruple hydrogen bonding.  Benefitting from the supramol. interactions, the fillers of SiO2-UPy are able to uniformly disperse and reduce the agglomeration.  As a result, the interphase between SiO2 and the polymer matrix, which is crucial to provide a fast conduction channel for lithium ions, is increased.  Consequently, the SHCPE exhibits an enhanced ionic cond. of 8.0 × 10-5 S cm-1 at 30 °C compared with that of the CPE blended with pristine SiO2.  More importantly, the self-healing and mech. properties of the SHCPE are simultaneously improved due to the increase of phys. cross-linked sites in the polymer matrix.  Owing to these outstanding properties, the lithium-ion batteries fabricated using the SHCPE exhibit stable cycling performance.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq5IWeu32WZ9rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh38HWIf6AIQw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXlslemt7k%253D&amp;md5=5a8a3e82e23329fac4b8bbfad7426852</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref24" class="rightTabRefNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit24" data-doi="10.1039/C8TA01907J"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ye, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Peng, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xie, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xue, Z.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Flexible, Self-Healing and Highly Stretchable Polymer Electrolyte via Quadruple Hydrogen Bonding for Lithium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Polymer electrolytes are envisioned to be promising alternatives to their liq. counterparts in lithium-ion batteries as they are able to address electrolyte inflammability and leakage issues.  However, polymer electrolytes usually suffer from cracks or breakage, which could lead to short circuits and bring severe safety issues.  Herein, we propose a paradigm to address these problems in which the polymer electrolyte is formed by a phys. cross-linked network via ureidopyrimidinone (UPy) contg. brush-like poly(ethylene glycol) chains.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Structural polymers are susceptible to damage in the form of cracks, which form deep within the structure where detection is difficult and repair is almost impossible.  Cracking leads to mech. degrdn. of fiber-reinforced polymer composites; in microelectronic polymeric components it can also lead to elec. failure.  Microcracking induced by thermal and mech. fatigue is also a long-standing problem in polymer adhesives.  Regardless of the application, once cracks have formed within polymeric materials, the integrity of the structure is significantly compromised.  Expts. exploring the concept of self-repair have been previously reported, but the only successful crack-healing methods that have been reported so far require some form of manual intervention.  Here, a structural polymeric material with the ability to autonomically heal cracks is reported.  The material incorporates a microencapsulated healing agent that is released upon crack intrusion.  Polymn. of the healing agent is then triggered by contact with an embedded catalyst, bonding the crack faces.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The poor elec. cond. and large volumetric changes hinder the practical application of red phosphorus despite its high theor. capacity of 2596 mA h g-1.  A simple approach to overcome this challenge via anchoring sol. lithium-sulfur intermediate compds. on the surface of red phosphorus and configuring an efficient interface is proposed here.  Sulfur is strategically incorporated into phosphorus by P-S bonding to construct a phosphorus-sulfur/carbon composite with a highly conductive interface.  The sol. Li-S intermediates formed during the electrochem. lithiation process could be anchored on the surface of phosphorus by capillary action to heal the cracks/defects of phosphorus caused by volumetric expansion.  Calcns. based on first principles demonstrate that the strategic introduction of sulfur enables band overlapping near the Fermi level, which facilitates high efficiency electron-transfer at the P-S interface and thus improves the overall electrochem. properties of the constructed composite.  Therefore, the phosphorus-sulfur/carbon composite delivers a high initial charge capacity of 1808.2 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 200 mA g-1 and maintains a reversible capacity of 935.3 mA h g-1 with a high coulombic efficiency of approx. 99.4% after 200 cycles.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoVxOgH7e1zFbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljKSsuPrSjGow"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXhvFymsbvO&amp;md5=cfbf006ba9af2c4deaf62388564c111c</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref28"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref28" class="rightTabRefNumLink">28</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit28" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201803854"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qian, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qi, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Mechanically Robust Atomic Oxygen-Resistant Coatings Capable of Autonomously Healing Damage in Low Earth Orbit Space Environment</span>. <i>Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Black phosphorus (BP) is a two-dimensional material potentially of great interest for applications in the fields of energy, sensing, and microelectronics.  One of the most interesting methods to obtain BP is the conversion from red phosphorus (RP) by means of high-energy mechanochem. synthesis.  To date, however, this synthesis process was not well characterized.  In this work, starting from the math. model of energy transfer during the ball milling process, we investigate the effects on RP → BP conversion of three exptl. parameters, the rotation speed, the milling time, and the wt. ratio between the spheres and the milled material (BtPw ratio).  The efficiency of the conversion process was verified by solid-state NMR, Raman spectroscopy, and X-ray diffraction.  Whereas the first two parameters have a minor importance, the BtPw ratio plays a primary role in the RP → BP conversion.  Yields approaching 100% can be obtained also with short milling times (15 min) and adequate rotation speed (e.g., 500 r.p.m.), provided that the BtPw ratio &gt;40:1 is used.  These results confirm the energy sustainability of the mechanochem. synthesis approach.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrSgBKOkouJiLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljKSsuPrSjGow"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXkt1Wgu74%253D&amp;md5=4bdff6829e6831fb76114d85e777b0eb</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref30"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref30" class="rightTabRefNumLink">30</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit30" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemmater.6b02982"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kwon, Y.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The strong dimerizing, quadruple hydrogen-bonding ureido-pyrimidone unit was used to obtain reversible polymer networks via a synthetic route from com. available starting materials.  The hydrogen-bonding ureido-pyrimidone network was prepd. using 3- and 4-isocyanatomethyl-1-methylcyclohexyl-isocyanate (IMCI) in the regioselective coupling reaction of multi-hydroxy functionalized ethylene oxide-propylene oxide copolymers with isocytosines.  1H- and 13C-NMR, IR, MS, and ES-MS anal., performed on a model reaction using butanol, demonstrated the formation of the hydrogen-bonding ureido-pyrimidone unit in a yield of more than 95%.  The well-defined, strong hydrogen-bonding ureido-pyrimidone network was compared with a traditional covalently bonded polymer network, a multi-directional hydrogen-bonded polymer network based on urea units, and a ref. compd.  The advantage of the reversible, hydrogen-bonded polymer networks is the formation of the thermodynamically most favorable products, which show a higher virtual mol. wt. and shear modulus, compared to the irreversible, covalently bonded polymer network.  The properties of the ureido-pyrimidone network are unique; the well-defined and strong dimerization of the ureido-pyrimidone unit does not require any addnl. stabilization such as crystn. or other kinds of phase sepn., and displays a well-defined viscoelastic transition.  The ureido-pyrimidone network represents the first example of a truly reversible polymer network showing these features.  Furthermore, the ureido-pyrimidone dimerization is strong enough to construct supramol. materials possessing acceptable mech. properties.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrDkfgzUZYtbbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhDwlxeUW4Pqw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaK1MXmt1Gkt74%253D&amp;md5=79d1707b3d2a94132abff851ad514575</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref36"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref36" class="rightTabRefNumLink">36</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit36" data-doi="10.1039/F29726800656"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Afifi-Effat, A.                         rightTab-cas_cit41R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">41</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Rheological properties and impedance spectroscopy of PMMA-PVdF blend and PMMA gel polymer electrolytes for advanced lithium batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Nicotera, I.; Coppola, L.; Oliviero, C.; Ranieri, G. A.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Ionics</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2005</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">11</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1 &amp; 2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">87-94</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">IONIFA</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Institute for Ionics</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Blended ionic conducting membranes formed by poly(Me methacrylate) (PMMA)/poly(vinylidene fluoride) (PVDF) (blend ratio PMMA/PVdF = 80/20), with LiClO4 as a salt and a mixt. of ethylene carbonate (EC) and propylene carbonate (PC) as plasticizer were prepd. and characterized by impedance spectroscopy and dynamic rheol. expts.  The results obtained on the blends are compared with those on PMMA gel-based polymer electrolytes incorporating the same EC/PC mixt. of plasticizer and the same quantity of salt.  The focus of this study was to illustrate the rheol. data of the gel and blend electrolytes and to show their mech. stability with temp. with respect to the technol. application.  Cond. values are reported in the 20-100° temp. range for different Li salt contents, while the rheol. behavior was recorded up to 140°.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp4ubI2AYVP47Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljoYCCBU9-2Iw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD2MXjslait7k%253D&amp;md5=43afb052852954d42e0832d0c8f72630</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref42"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref42" class="rightTabRefNumLink">42</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit42" data-doi="10.1122/1.549853"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Winter, H. H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chambon, F.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Analysis of Linear Viscoelasticity of a Crosslinking Polymer at the Gel Point</span>. <i>J. Rheol.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">1986</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>30</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">367</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">382</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1122/1.549853</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit42R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">42</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Analysis of linear viscoelasticity of a crosslinking polymer at the gel point</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Winter, H. Henning; Chambon, Francois</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Rheology (New York, NY, United States)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1986</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">30</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">367-82</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JORHD2</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0148-6055</span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">A very simple memory integral constitutive equation is suggested for the stress in crosslinking polymers at their transition from liq. to solid state (gel point).  The equation allows for only a single material parameter, the strength (Pa s1/2 units), and it is able to describe every known viscoelastic phenomenon at the gel point.  Measurements are performed on vinyl-terminated di-Me siloxane model networks with balanced stoichiometry for which the crosslinking reaction is stopped at different degrees of conversion.  At the gel point, the loss and storage moduli are congruent and proportional to ω1/2 over a wide range of temp. (-50 to +180°) and 5 decases of frequency ω.  The hypothesis is made that this behavior is valid in the entire range 0 &lt; ω &lt; ∞.  This congruence hypothesis is consistent with the Kramers-Kronig relation and leads to a constitutive equation which shows that congruent functions G'(ω) = G''(ω) (G' = storage modulus, G'' = loss modulus) are as much a rheol. property at the gel point as are infinite viscosity and zero equil. modulus.  This makes it now possible to measure exactly the instant of gelation of a crosslinking polymer without having to stop the crosslinking reaction.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqjBseBOfpNfbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljoYCCBU9-2Iw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaL28Xks1Wgsbs%253D&amp;md5=e06030c60b008c8c9dde1165ce2bd68d</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref43"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref43" class="rightTabRefNumLink">43</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit43" data-doi="10.1021/ja01619a008"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Williams, M. L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Landel, R. F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ferry, J. D.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">The Temperature Dependence of Relaxation Mechanisms in Amorphous Polymers and Other Glass-Forming Liquids</span>. <i>J. Am. Chem. Soc.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">1955</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>77</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">3701</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">3707</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/ja01619a008</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit43R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">43</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The temperature dependence of relaxation mechanisms in amorphous polymers and other glass-forming liquids</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Williams, Malcolm L.; Landel, Robert F.; Ferry, John D.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1955</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">77</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3701-7</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JACSAT</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0002-7863</span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">An empirical function, aT, is used to describe the temp. dependence of all mech. and elec. relaxation processes for amorphous polymers above their glass transition temps., Tg.  If a suitable reference temp., Ts, is chosen for each system and if log aT is plotted against T - Ts, curves are shown to coincide for the polymers, organic glass-forming liquids, and inorganic glasses investigated.  For polyisobutylene and polystyrene polymers of varying mol. wt., Ts - Tg is const.  Data in the literature were used to calc. aT from the relation, aT = ηTsρs/ηsT, where η is viscosity and ρ is d.  The org. liquids studied were glucose, abietic acid, dimethylthianthrene, PrOH, propylene glycol, and glycerol.  B2O3 and 3 optical glasses were compared.  For a series of polymers, Ts - Tg = 50 ± 5°.  Empirically, the relation, log aT = -17.44(T - Tg)/(51.6 + T - Tg), holds.  The expression of the temp. dependence of relaxation mechanisms in terms of an apparent activation energy has limited usefulness.  A theoretical relation derived for aT is: log aT = -(1/2.303fg)(T - Tg)/(fg/α2 + T - Tg), where fg is the fractional free vol at Tg and α2 is the difference between the thermal expansion coeffs. above and below Tg.  The equation is identical in form to the empirical relation.  Values of α2 and fg were 4.8 × 10-4/degree and 0.025, resp.  The value of α2 agrees with measured values for several amorphous polymers, and the value of fg appears to be reasonable.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqJmq_dRK1DdrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljoYCCBU9-2Iw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaG2MXnt1CltQ%253D%253D&amp;md5=27dd24647e57faeea98547f6b0e4503f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref44"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref44" class="rightTabRefNumLink">44</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit44" data-doi="10.1016/j.ssi.2003.09.007"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nicotera, I.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Coppola, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Oliviero, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Russo, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ranieri, G. A.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Some Physicochemical Properties of PAN-Based Electrolytes: Solution and Gel Microstructures</span>. <i>Solid State Ionics</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2004</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>167</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">213</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">220</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.ssi.2003.09.007</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit44R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">44</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Some physicochemical properties of PAN-based electrolytes: solution and gel microstructures</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Nicotera, Isabella; Coppola, Luigi; Oliviero, Cesare; Russo, Alessandro; Ranieri, Giuseppe Antonio</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Solid State Ionics</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Science B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The mech. and electrochem. properties of gel electrolytes, formed by immobilizing ethylene carbonate (EC)-propylene carbonate (PC)/lithium perchlorate (LiClO4) solns. in a polyacrylonitrile (PAN) matrix, were studied by oscillating rheol. expts., cond. measurements and 1H-NMR spectroscopy.  The gel's rheol. behavior showed two thermo-reversible structural transitions in the temp. range 25-120 °C independent from the salt content in the membranes but can be attributed to the only polymeric matrix.  The authors also report a study of the EC-PC/LiClO4 solns. in a wide concn. range performed by pulsed gradient spin-echo NMR (PGSE-NMR) technique.  The solvent self-diffusion coeff. measurements have allowed elaborating a model to describe the solvation mechanism of the lithium ions in these solns.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrpDiIZZuHy3rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljoYCCBU9-2Iw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD2cXjtF2qtr8%253D&amp;md5=ab3268fccc8313efa11e9a744b45aa6a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref45"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref45" class="rightTabRefNumLink">45</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit45" data-doi="10.1002/masy.200750132"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nicotera, I.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Coppola, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Oliviero, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ranieri, G. A.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Spectromechanical Properties of Polymeric Gel Electrolytes and Blends</span>. <i>Macromol.                 10.1002/masy.200750132</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit45R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">45</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Spectromechanical properties of polymeric gel electrolytes and blends</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Nicotera, I.; Coppola, L.; Oliviero, C.; Ranieri, G. A.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Macromolecular Symposia</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2007</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">247</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">Times of Polymers and Composites</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">282-294</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">MSYMEC</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">In this paper we studied the spectro-mech. properties of polymer gel electrolytes and blends by means of shear rheol.  These electrolytes were obtained by immobilizing EC/PC/LiClO4 solns. in a polymeric matrix of PEO, PAN and PMMA, resp.  Two structural and thermo-reversible transitions, a strong-to-weak gel and a gel-to-sol, were revealed and discussed.  Moreover, PMMA-PVdF blends have been studied as a function of the different polymeric ratios in order to obtain the best compromise between ionic conduction and mech. properties.  Interesting mech. properties were obsd. at some intermediate blend compns.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGquFqFaAZOKMLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljoYCCBU9-2Iw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD2sXjslWqu7Y%253D&amp;md5=e189c21021f532d8796028fcc1d95fc9</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref46"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref46" class="rightTabRefNumLink">46</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit46" data-doi="10.1039/C9EE00283A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cho, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kang, Y.-M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Critical Design Factors for Kinetically Favorable P-Based Compounds toward Alloying with Na Ions for High-Power Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Energy Environ. Sci.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>12</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1326</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">1333</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/C9EE00283A</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit46R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">46</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Critical design factors for kinetically favorable P-based compounds toward alloying with Na ions for high-power sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Duho; Zhang, Kai; Cho, Maenghyo; Kang, Yong-Mook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">12</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1326-1333</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">First-principles calcns. with exptl. validation enable us to radically predict and compare the electron transport, elastic softness, and thermodn. phase stabilities of black phosphorus (BP) and GeP3 to figure out the crucial factors for designing the most kinetically favorable P-based compds. toward alloying with Na ions.  From the viewpoint of electron transport, Ge is able to provide P with electrons due to the difference of electronegativity, causing GeP3 to have a significantly reduced band gap and a lower electron-hopping barrier compared to BP.  Because the structure of GeP3 looks more elastically softened than that of BP, its Na+ migration barriers in the sodiated states must be lower than those of the sodiated BP structure as proven by the higher Na+ diffusion coeffs. of GeP3.  The thermodn. phase stabilities of BP and GeP3 could be predicted by calcg. their mixing enthalpy values and thereby homogeneous bulk free energies were obtained for Na1-x(GeP3) (0.0 ≤ x ≤ 0.5) indicating that no phase sepn. occurs in the sodiated GeP3 because of an addnl. stable single-phase at x = 0.25.  By contrast, the sodiated P easily gets sepd. into NaP and Na0.5P with the same sodiation content.  The suppressed phase sepn. in GeP3 makes the Na+ distribution homogeneous as confirmed by TEM-EDS color mapping and thus contributes to increasing Na+ diffusion coeffs. because Na+ migration can be primarily interrupted by the interfaces or grain boundaries between the sepd. phases.  Taking into account three enhanced physicochem. properties, the GeP3 electrodes may well present a superior rate capability to the BP electrode, underscoring that electron transport, elastic softness, and thermodn. phase stabilities must be the most important design factors for realizing kinetically favorable P-based compds. toward alloying with Na+.  In detail, even at an ultrahigh c.d. of 20 A g-1, the capacity of the GeP3 electrode still comes to 0.197 A h g-1.  At 1 A g-1, GeP3 could maintain a discharge capacity of 0.499 A h g-1 even after 1000 cycles.  This study may provide promising guidelines for the design of alloy-based electrode materials for high power and energy SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpuohmt-cRMxrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljoYCCBU9-2Iw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXjt1ertrk%253D&amp;md5=e99bce6c29c7ff032153d09282e7b068</span></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i17">Supporting Information</h2></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.0c22464?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.0c22464</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Raman Spectra, SEM, and XRD of BP;         <script nonce="90da87218abfb325-SJC">
(function(){
	
	/*Event Tracking for RIS menu dropdown - Article pages*/

	$('.cit-download-dropdown_button').on('click',function(e)
	{	
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	}); 

 	$('.header_contnav-pre a').on('click', function(e){
	    e.preventDefault();
	     gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Left Arrow'});
	}); 

	$('.share_button').on('click', function(e){
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Share'});
	});

	$("#pane-m1con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Figures'});
	});

	$("#pane-m2con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'References'});
	});

	$("#pane-m3con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Supporting Info'});
	});

	$('.tab__close').on('click', function(){
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Close'});
	}); 



})();

</script>

        <span class="system-message-date">Thursday, February 06, 2025</span>
        <p>Please be aware that pubs.acs.org is undergoing maintenance from Saturday February 1 to Monday Febraury 3, that may have an impact on your experience. <script nonce="90da87218abfb325-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da87218abfb325-SJC">
"use strict"
//Script checks gdpr cookie and submits form
	/* 
	Create an in-page pop-up message, only for the InfoCentral homepage, that will gather email registrants for the library marketing list.
	 
	Needs the end point
	*/

var infoCentralCapture = (function() {

    //Detect mobile device
    window.mobilecheck = function() {
        var check = false;
        (function(a){if(/(android|bb\d+|meego).+mobile|avantgo|bada\/|blackberry|blazer|compal|elaine|fennec|hiptop|iemobile|ip(hone|od)|iris|kindle|lge |maemo|midp|mmp|mobile.+firefox|netfront|opera m(ob|in)i|palm( os)?|phone|p(ixi|re)\/|plucker|pocket|psp|series(4|6)0|symbian|treo|up\.(browser|link)|vodafone|wap|windows ce|xda|xiino/i.test(a)||/1207|6310|6590|3gso|4thp|50[1-6]i|770s|802s|a wa|abac|ac(er|oo|s\-)|ai(ko|rn)|al(av|ca|co)|amoi|an(ex|ny|yw)|aptu|ar(ch|go)|as(te|us)|attw|au(di|\-m|r |s )|avan|be(ck|ll|nq)|bi(lb|rd)|bl(ac|az)|br(e|v)w|bumb|bw\-(n|u)|c55\/|capi|ccwa|cdm\-|cell|chtm|cldc|cmd\-|co(mp|nd)|craw|da(it|ll|ng)|dbte|dc\-s|devi|dica|dmob|do(c|p)o|ds(12|\-d)|el(49|ai)|em(l2|ul)|er(ic|k0)|esl8|ez([4-7]0|os|wa|ze)|fetc|fly(\-|_)|g1 u|g560|gene|gf\-5|g\-mo|go(\.w|od)|gr(ad|un)|haie|hcit|hd\-(m|p|t)|hei\-|hi(pt|ta)|hp( i|ip)|hs\-c|ht(c(\-| |_|a|g|p|s|t)|tp)|hu(aw|tc)|i\-(20|go|ma)|i230|iac( |\-|\/)|ibro|idea|ig01|ikom|im1k|inno|ipaq|iris|ja(t|v)a|jbro|jemu|jigs|kddi|keji|kgt( |\/)|klon|kpt |kwc\-|kyo(c|k)|le(no|xi)|lg( g|\/(k|l|u)|50|54|\-[a-w])|libw|lynx|m1\-w|m3ga|m50\/|ma(te|ui|xo)|mc(01|21|ca)|m\-cr|me(rc|ri)|mi(o8|oa|ts)|mmef|mo(01|02|bi|de|do|t(\-| |o|v)|zz)|mt(50|p1|v )|mwbp|mywa|n10[0-2]|n20[2-3]|n30(0|2)|n50(0|2|5)|n7(0(0|1)|10)|ne((c|m)\-|on|tf|wf|wg|wt)|nok(6|i)|nzph|o2im|op(ti|wv)|oran|owg1|p800|pan(a|d|t)|pdxg|pg(13|\-([1-8]|c))|phil|pire|pl(ay|uc)|pn\-2|po(ck|rt|se)|prox|psio|pt\-g|qa\-a|qc(07|12|21|32|60|\-[2-7]|i\-)|qtek|r380|r600|raks|rim9|ro(ve|zo)|s55\/|sa(ge|ma|mm|ms|ny|va)|sc(01|h\-|oo|p\-)|sdk\/|se(c(\-|0|1)|47|mc|nd|ri)|sgh\-|shar|sie(\-|m)|sk\-0|sl(45|id)|sm(al|ar|b3|it|t5)|so(ft|ny)|sp(01|h\-|v\-|v )|sy(01|mb)|t2(18|50)|t6(00|10|18)|ta(gt|lk)|tcl\-|tdg\-|tel(i|m)|tim\-|t\-mo|to(pl|sh)|ts(70|m\-|m3|m5)|tx\-9|up(\.b|g1|si)|utst|v400|v750|veri|vi(rg|te)|vk(40|5[0-3]|\-v)|vm40|voda|vulc|vx(52|53|60|61|70|80|81|83|85|98)|w3c(\-| )|webc|whit|wi(g |nc|nw)|wmlb|wonu|x700|yas\-|your|zeto|zte\-/i.test(a.substr(0,4))) check = true;})(navigator.userAgent||navigator.vendor||window.opera);
              return check;
        };

    var _isMobile = window.mobilecheck();
    if (_isMobile) {
        $('#gdpr-message-modal').addClass('mobile');
    }

    var emailValid = "",
        time = 3000,
        btnStatus = true,
        errorStatus = false,
        errormessage = "<p class='gdpr_err'><b>Please Enter a valid email address<b><p>";

    function init() {
        setTimeout(function() {
            var cookieExist = getCookie("gdpr");
            if (cookieExist) {
                //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");
                return false;
            } else {
                //console.log("gdpr cookie does not exist")
                gdprMessage();
            }
            //Events
            $('#closebtn').on("click", hideForm);

            //Handle submit
            $('#gdpr-message-btn').on("click", function(event) {
                handleClick();
            });

            // //Hide if clicked outside div - TO DO stop handling click after submit
            // $(document).click(function(event) {
            // 	event.stopPropagation();
            //   console.log(event.target);
            //   if (!$(event.target).closest("#gdpr-message-content").length) {
            //     $("body").find("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
            //     $(this).off();
            //   }
            // });
            $('#gdpr-con-btn').click(function() {
                setCookie("gdpr", "true");
                hideForm();
            });
        }, time);
    }

    //Show the message
    function gdprMessage() {
        //console.log("Show GDPR Message");
        var tMessage, emailInput, messageButton, ewrap;

        $('#gdpr-message-modal').show();
    }

    function handleClick() {
        //console.log($("#gdpr-input").val());
        emailValid = validateEmail($("#gdpr-input").val());
        if (emailValid) {
            //Submit the form
            //Hide form
            if ($('.gdpr_err')) {
                $('.gdpr_err').hide();
            }

            $("#gdpr-message-content")
                //If successful then...
            setCookie("gdpr", "true");
            //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid + " submiting form");
        } else {
            //Show error message
            gdprErrorMessage();
            //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid);
        }
    }

    function gdprErrorMessage() {
        if (errorStatus) {
            return false;
        } else {
            errorStatus = true;
            $("#gdpr-message-content").append(errormessage);
        }
    }

    function validateEmail(email) {
        var re = /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
        return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
    }

    function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
    }

    function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + '=([^;]+)'));
        if (match) return match[1];
    }

    function hideForm() { //And set cookie
        $('#gdpr-message-modal').hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");
        gtag('event', 'GDPR Modal', { 'event_category': 'Pubs GDPR', 'event_label':'GDPR Dismissal'});
    }

    return {
        init: init
    }
})();

$(document).ready(infoCentralCapture.init);
</script>


                    
                        



        
        <script nonce="90da87218abfb325-SJC">
var currentJournal = "ACS Applied Materials &amp;amp; Interfaces";
$(".citationLinks > a").click(function(e){
    if($(this).text() === "ACS Full Text ") {
        gtag('event', 'ACS Full Text', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Crossref") {
        gtag('event', 'Crossref', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "CAS") {
        gtag('event', 'CAS', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
        gtag('event', 'PubMed', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Google Scholar") {
        gtag('event', 'Google Scholar', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).hasClass("issue-item_open-url") === true) {
        gtag('event', 'OpenURL', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
});
</script>

                    
                        



        
        <script nonce="90da87218abfb325-SJC">
$(document).ready(function(){
    var cookie = "";
    var uri = "/doi/full/10.1021/html_original_uri?id=123";
    console.log("ACSPubs2 start");
    $.getJSON('https://extreme-ip-lookup.com/json/', function(data, status) {
            if ( status == 'success' ) {
                $.each(data, function(i, field){
                    cookie=cookie.concat(i+' = '+field+" - ");
                });
                
            } else {
                    cookie=status;
            }

            cookie1 = cookie;
            cookie = cookie1 + "; path=/; domain=.acs.org";
            //document.cookie = "ACSPubs2= "+ btoa(cookie) ;
            $.cookie("ACSPubs2", btoa(cookie), {path: '/'}); // Session cookie valid across the entire site.
            //TODO send cookie val to the controller for logging
            console.log("ACSPubs2 success");
    })
    .fail(function(xx, status, err){
        cookie="error: 127.0.0.1";
        //document.cookie = "ACSPubs2= "+ btoa(cookie) ;
        $.cookie("ACSPubs2", btoa(cookie), {path: '/'}); // Session cookie valid across the entire site.
        console.log("ACSPubs2 failed");
        //TODO send cookie val to the controller for logging
    });
});    
</script>
